B000000WFF|5|The Spice Girls, those glitter-besotted pottymouth disciples of mayhem, continued their history-making bubblegum crusade against the decadent relics of grunge on this, the second of two albums that would kick in a new pop era of bouncy, glowing optimism and divinely frivolous tunes.  But none who came after could reach the fever-pitch exhilaration they managed on song after song.  \\"Spiceworld\\" takes off on a euphoric trip through the archetypal pop formats-- Motown, disco, even showtunes--to create a global groove for the dancing masses of teens, tweens, kids and adults who all fell under the spell of these fiercely independent pop princesses.  \\"Spice Up Your Life\\" swirls with carnaval percussion and a wriggling, jungle-inspired beat; its an Oasis-like wall of sound built out of Latin rhythms and the patented Spice hooks.  \\"Stop\\" is their inspired Supremes homage, dizzying in its joyous recreation of that classic sound.  \\"Too Much\\" is the first brooding ballad, more jazzy than \\"2 Become One\\" but similarly lush and lovestruck-- just listen and consider how very, very far everyone else on the scene is from creating slow tunes with such mastery.  \\"Saturday Night Divas\\" slinks along with a dazzlingly orchestrated syncopation.  The floor-stomping \\"Never Give Up On the Good Times\\" represents a pinnacle of energy, even on this disc of flushed-cheek dance tunes-- this disco anthem shakes and shimmys in  ecstatic release, the explosion of positve energy that is the Spice Girls' trademark.  And on and on and on.  \\"Move Over\\" punkishly extends the Pepsi-commercial theme into a song on Girl Power revolution.  \\"Do It\\" lays down the feminist 'rules are for fools' theme, with a swooningly delicious bridge sweeter than melted ice cream (thanks especially to Emma on that one).  \\"Denying\\" is a sultrier sort of R&B tune, somewhat akin to the \\"Forever\\" album's material with its smoldering lover's anger.  On the second ballad, \\"Viva Forever\\", the Spice Girls express a shockingly Neo-Platonic conception of life (\\"Live forever, for the moment/ Ever searching, for the one\\") in dreamy lyrics that express fervent yearning,  over a swelling orchestral arrangement with touches of Spanish flavor.  Yes, and then there's the closing number, \\"Lady is a Vamp\\" where the Spice Girls salute their culture-heroes in a rousing, comic piece of burlesque.  Whether as solo artists or a group, we'll certainly continue to enjoy the show.  \\"Spiceworld\\", in each and every song, is a monument in pop history, an artifact as enduring as \\"A Hard Day's Night\\" or the \\"Immaculate Collection\\".  As writers and performers, the Spice Girls achieved new heights of energetic bliss, and \\"Spiceworld\\" remains a must for all who would know how great pop can be.

052594527X|5|This is the kind of first novel many serious writers ache to produce.  Chevalier effectively takes us away from the hustle and noise of the 21st Century--the omnipresent cell phones, the whirring faxes, the beeping  answering machines--into a world rich in silence and texture.  Practically  from the beginning the reader will find herself unconsciously relaxing as  Chevalier quickly brings 17th Century Delft to life: the silence of  darkened rooms, the crackle from a kitchen fireplace, the muffled sounds of  feet moving swiftly up stone stairs.<br />The heroine, Griet, is an  intelligent 16 year old  who, because of a family tragedy, is ripped from  her poor but closely knit family to work as a maid for artist Johannes  Vermeer's household.  Unlike her own home's rather straightforward traumas  (how to survive now that her father, a tilemaker, has been blinded in an  industrial accident), the superficially quiet Vermeer household seethes  with  jealously, turmoil, intrigue and secrets.  Griet comes as a maid, but  she evolves into a painter's assistant and, ultimately, muse--a  transformation that changes the direction she may otherwise have chosen for  herself.<br />Chevalier brilliantly and persuasively describes the rigor and  tedium required to maintain a 17th Century home; just reading through  Griet's daily workload is enough to make one want to take a nap.  The  divison between server and served, the fine line Griet must walk to please  her insecure new mistress and her perfidious children, make modern  sensibilities bridle.  Life, as John Kennedy liked to observe, is not fair.   And as this book matter of factly points out, it was even less so before  democracy's leavening influence.<br />My biggest regret re Chevalier's book  was that there wasn't more of it.  There didn't need to be, but I was  sorry, even with its bittersweet ending, that the book had come to an end.

0393317552|4|Carnage and Culture: Landmark Battles in the Rise of Western Power by Victor Davis Hanson is an interesting read.  The central thesis of the book is that the Western style of direct decisive infantry warfare, with roots in democracy and capitalism, has been historically superior and partly responsible for the Western Worlds superior position.  Hanson seeks to present his book as a kind of rebuttal to Guns, Germs, and Steel: The Fates of Human Societies by Jared Diamond.  <br />As to the latter objective. Hanson fails miserably for Hanson's and Diamond's theories are not inconsistent and Hanson's attempt to make them so detracts from the rest of the book..  Diamond's seeks to explain why it was the Spanish who conquered the Incas in 16th century Peru and not Incas who sailed to Madrid to conquer the Spanish.  He theorizes that the Spanish had greater access to domesticatable crops and animals and European geography allowed for an easier exchange of ideas from other burgeoning societies.  Hanson seeks to minimize the advantages of what  Diamond speaks of and instead focuses on military strategy, and western economic and political thought.  What Hanson misses is that without advanced military transport in the horse, the availability of sophisticated weapons, such as steel swords, rudimentary guns and cannon the Incas would never have been conquered.<br />Hanson's own treatment of the conquest of Tenochtitlan (Mexico City) in 1520-1521 is the proof.  The Mexican Army utilized infantry tactics, since they did not have any calvary.  They fought decisive battles with their opponents, since like the ancient Greeks they had a war season.  While the Greeks was based on the harvest, the Mexican was based on the harvest and religious considerations.  While they also fought symbolic flower wars these were not the ones fought to protect their Empire.<br />The Spanish, with several hundred Spaniards and thousands of natives under Cortez, were able to defeat the Mexicans.  It was the horses, guns and eventually smallpox which were decisive. There can be no argument that Spain or Cortez operated in a democratic or that capitalism was the basis of their economy. One can even argue that what defeated the Aztecs was their penchant for human sacrifice and the desire to capture Cortez instead of killing him.  In numerous battles the Aztecs could have killed Cortez but did not.  <br />Hanson's and Diamond's book build on each other and offer a compelling explanation to justify each others theories.  Hanson unintentionally builds on Diamond's concepts to explain why societies with equal resources do not necessarily develop equally.<br />Otherwise, Hanson's book is well written and very interesting.  It is in the description of the battles and the campaigns where he excels.  It would have been better if Hanson had dealt with some of the possible exceptions to his theory - the century old Ottoman Empire, the supremacy of the Prussian military tradition which was hardly based on notions of democracy and the Russian almost suicidal defense of their homeland at Stalingrad.  Maybe he is saving that for his next book.

B006BG7RI0|5|\\"Casablanca\\" is a motion picture classic beloved by millions.  Warner Home Video has honored this wartime romantic drama with an elaborate box set to commemorate the 70th anniversary of its theatrical release.<br /><br />World War II is raging and the Nazis are occupying North Africa, but Rick Blaine (Humphrey Bogart) is sitting out the war in Casablanca as proprietor of the most popular nightclub in town.  The cynical Rick comes into possession of two letters of transit permitting their carriers to leave the country.  To placate Nazi Major Strasser (Conrad Veidt), local police Captain Renault (Claude Rains) detains Czech underground leader Victor Laszlo (Paul Henreid), who is accompanied by Ilsa (Ingrid Bergman), Rick's former love.  Old flames are rekindled and Rick plans use the letters of transit to leave Casablanca with Ilsa.<br /><br />The film could have turned out to be a standard wartime drama, but everything clicked:  Michael Curtiz's direction; a fresh and clever script; colorful characters; the chemistry between Bogart and Bergman; a first-class supporting cast (Rains, Peter Lorre, Sydney Greenstreet, Dooley Wilson); a haunting song, \\"As Time Goes By,\\" that runs through the film as Rick and Ilsa's love theme; and the immediacy of a war whose outcome was still undetermined.<br /><br />Humphrey Bogart, who had previously played gangsters, took on his first romantic lead in \\"Casablanca.\\"  George Raft had unsuccessfully angled for the role of Rick.  Ingrid Bergman, under contract at the time to David O. Selznick, was borrowed by Jack Warner to co-star with Bogart.  Composer Max Steiner was against using \\"As Time Goes By\\" and Warner finally agreed to let him write an original song for the movie, but by then Bergman had cut her hair for another movie and couldn't re-shoot the scenes in which the song was heard.  Sam, Rick's singer-pianist, who performs the song, was nearly made a female, with Hazel Scott, Lena Horne, and Ella Fitzgerald considered for the role.  Dooley Wilson, the eventual Sam, was a drummer and faked playing the piano.<br /><br />No movie has more memorable dialogue.  \\"Here's looking at you, kid\\" was voted as the Number 5 most famous movie quote by the American Film Institute.  Other lines that live in memory include \\"We'll always have Paris,\\" \\"Round up the usual suspects,\\" \\"The problems of two people don't amount to a hill of beans in this world,\\" and \\"I think this is the beginning of a beautiful friendship.\\"  But Bogie never spoke the line \\"Play it again Sam.\\"  That was the title of Woody Allen's 1972 homage to \\"Casablanca.\\"  Rather, Rick says to Sam, who had performed the song at Ilsa's request, \\"You played it for her.  You can play it for me.\\"<br /><br />The 3-Disc 70th Anniversary Edition box set contains Blu-ray and DVD versions, reproduction of the original 1942 French film poster, 60-page production art book, and collectible drink coaster set.  Among the 14 hours of bonus material are two new documentaries: \\"Casablanca: An Unlikely Classic\\" and a look at the career of director Michael Curtiz; full-length documentaries on the history of Warner Brothers Studio and its founders; outtakes; a vintage cartoon; two radio broadcasts; several short subjects; audio commentary by Roger Ebert; and an introduction by Lauren Bacall.

B000003TAL|5|Daydream Nation is regarded as one of the most groundbreaking and influential indie records of the 80's. When you listen to this album, it's easy to hear why. All through the album you can hear a large number of sound layers consisting of guitars with alternate tunings and lots of feedback. This may sound as if Daydream Nation is just some sort of experimental record that you problably won't like. In fact, Daydream Nation is not an experimental record at all. Through every song you can hear a very clear pattern and the alternate tunings and sometimes heavy feedback just add something extra to all of the songs. This is what makes Sonic Youth such a unique band and this is what made them so influential to so many other bands such as Nirvana. Just listen to Daydream Nation first and then listen once more to any of the early Nirvana records and you'll hear what i mean.  <br /> <br />P.s. I saw them live last monday at the Paradiso in Amsterdam and it's just amazing to see how skillfull they are when it comes to handling their instruments. Thurston Moore is simply one of the most genius guitar players around.  Anyway, check out Daydream Nation, you won't be dissapointed.

B003VANOFY|5|First of all I would like to address all of the one and two star reviews that I read before I purchased this product. Many of them complain about things not working, especially with Windows 7 64bit, however, none of these problems are an issue anymore. Many of the dates on those reviews are from 2009-2010 but Logitech as continued to release new drivers that fix issues. With that being said, do not even bother with the CD in the package as that drivers on that are completely outdated. The CD has version 1.0.xxx, while the latest driver can be downloaded from Logitech directly and at the time of this review is 8.30.86 (See link at end of review). A notably big difference from 1.0. Also disregard any reviews that claim the driver is no longer supported as the latest driver was posted on 06/05/2012.    Feature Review:    -G Keys: 3 Fully programmable G-Keys. The software gives them lots of functionality. Currently I have them setup with teamspeak. Some said this was not possible but it is simple to setup. Just go into teamspeak, go to shortcut keys and set a key combo. Then in the Logitech software set a G-Key to that key combo. Simple really.    -EQ: Nothing more you can ask for. There is a fully 10 channel graphic EQ as well as a simple two channel EQ. To access the graphic EQ click the \\">\\" on the right side of the two channel EQ at the bottom and then \\"Turn on The Advanced EQ.\\"    -7.1 Surround Sound: Works very well. As good as you can expect from a headset. Do note did exceed my expectations. When using the surround sound make sure you flip the switch on the back of the left ear that enables surround sound. I would recommend running the included 7.1 surround sound test.    -Voice Morphing: A novelty really but it works. I don't plan on using it but all the voice did work.    -Wireless Range: Better than I expected! Works through walls and in different rooms as advertised.    -Mic Sensitivity: INCREDIBLE. You will defiantly need to adjust the sensitivity in windowsand other apps that you use. The mic was able to record me whispering \\"1,2,3\\" as quietly as I could so it hears everything.    -Speakers and Noise Canceling: Works well. It does not block 100% of sound but nevertheless it is effective at blocking most sound and focusing your attention on what is playing on the headset.    Other Features I liked:  I love the volume control and mute buttons. The volume control is digital and actually controls windows volume which really impressed me and is a really useful feature. I also love the red LED on the mic boom that indicates if the mic is muted and in case anyone was wondering the mic does mute itself when it is placed in the upright position.    I was also very impressed with the cable management in the wired usb connector. The cable spins up tightly and locks into place (hard to explain but trust me it is very nice). Also the usb receiver plugs into this meaning you only plug in one usb to the back of your computer and you are set for charging and operation.    Good batery life and short(ish) charge time.    The packaging should win an award!    Cons:    -Did not come charged at all    -***The only problem I had with this device is when I was first trying to get it working I had one small problem. The driver was not working and none of the buttons on the headset did anything. If you have this problem where nothing seems to work (easy to know if you have this problem if the red mic mute LED is not coming on) just exit the driver software and turn off the headset. Then start the driver software and THEN turn on the headset. This worked for me. If this does not work for you try turning the headset off and then back on while the driver software is still running.    Overall:    A really wonderful product that I highly recommend!!! B000000YG5|5|The question of who was the greater tenor sax player--Rollins or John Coltrane--continues even today. Let's look at it this way...in 1956, Coltrane was gaining prominence in Miles Davis' quintet, but he was far from being a great player. He did not yet have the technique to express the wild ideas in his brain. A year later, however, he went to play with Thelonious Monk's quartet, and the experience liberated him and his style. Only at that point could he truly hold his own against Rollins, who was already a mature player by 1956. Compared side by side, it seems Coltrane recorded more bona fide classic albums than Rollins, but I don't believe any of them are as consistently great as &quot;Saxophone Colossus&quot; (&quot;Giant Steps&quot; comes closest). Rollins' playing throughout the album is extraordinary, especially on the last number, &quot;Blue 7&quot;, a strange blues improvisation which features one of Max Roach's fiercest drum solos. Tommy Flanagan plays piano, and he is known to double the quality of any album he plays on. Doug Watkins plays the bass, and he's got a nice sense of swing...one wonders why he only solos on one tune--&quot;Moritat&quot;. But the star is Rollins, who is on fire from the word go. Clarity, coherence, speed, imagination and unpredictability are his weapons, and they make this probably the best saxophone record ever.

B00006F7S4|5|In the world of Beck, change is a way of life. He's covered pretty much every major genre over the course of his career and never seems to miss a beat. And, for me, there's always something to like about whatever Beck's doing, although not everyone probably feels the same. He's been knocked because of his many times ironic lyrics or for the fact that he seems to change his musical arena every few years; he says he makes whatever kind of record that everyone else isn't making, which is probably the most true. Alas, Beck was supposed to making a rock record, but that's nothing like the Beck we get this time. This is sad, melancholy Beck, and very straightforward, truthful Beck and I got to admit I like this Beck as much, or more, than the previous ones.<br />'Sea Change' is pretty much in it's entirety full of glum musings concerning the breakup he experienced with his long-time girlfriend. Of course, song titles like &quot;Guess I'm Doin' Fine,&quot; &quot;Lonesome Tears,&quot; &quot;Lost Cause,&quot; and &quot;Already Dead&quot; kind of lead you to fact that this record probably isn't all that perky. The thing that for me really amplifies this to a very touching sadness is the way Beck delivers his vocals with a somber, heartfelt tone that really shows depth which maybe wasn't there before on previous records. Overall, it's really quite melodic and beautiful.<br />This record is kind of a sibling record of 1998's 'Mutations' in that it's basically acoustic oriented. Another similarity to the record is that Nigel Godrich is back as the producer and wraps Beck's sound in a nice array of strings, effects, and bleeps that keep each song fresh and adds an uplifting vibe in Beck's bare somber tones . On songs such as &quot;Paper Tiger&quot; and &quot;Round the Bend&quot;, Godrich layers a orchestrations that really make the song sound complete and make songs that could have ended up somewhat ordinary, extraordinary. Throughout the record it seems that Beck and Godrich were able to decipher what each song needed to compliment Beck's voice and guitar, such as the effects on &quot;Lost Cause&quot;, or if the song needed anything at all (&quot;Already Dead&quot;).<br />Is this Beck's best record yet? Possibly, and I'm really beginning to think it may be. This album is both immediately accessible and able to grow to display depth on further listens. All in all, he's created what may be the ultimate breakup record with it's themes of loss and sadness, but with a feeling of moving forward and letting the past be the past.e able to decipher what each song needed to compliment Beck's voice and guitar, such as the effects on &quot;Lost Cause&quot;, or if the song needed anything at all (&quot;Already Dead&quot;).<br />Is this Beck's best record yet? B00005JLR8|4|This film focuses on Sarah and Johnny Sullivan as they relocate with their two daughters from Ireland through Canada to New York City's Hell's Kitchen. Once in residence in a squalid tenement, they begin a new life together with high hopes and great expectations, although haunted by the death of a toddler. That loss presumably motivated Sarah (Samantha Morton) and Johnny (Paddy Considine) to make a fresh start in a strange city where so many other immigrant families have also done so for more than a century. Johnny is an aspiring actor whose ambitions are endured by his generally sullen wife. It soon becomes obvious that Sarah and (especially) Johnny are ill-prepared for the responsibilities of parenthood. Violent crime as well as alcohol and drug abuse surround the Sullivans where they live and yet the two daughters are often allowed to roam around unsupervised.<br />Over time, the Sullivans become friends with Mateo (Djimon Hounsou), a neighbor in the tenement who eventually provides unexpected assistance when it is needed most. (The nature of that assistance is best revealed in the film.) Frankly, I still do not know what to make of the relationship between Mateo and the Sullivans. The friendship they share was more a distraction than a revelation as I struggled to understand Sheridan's purposes. Unlike Boo Radley in To Kill a Mockingbird, for example, Mateo seems a contrivance.<br />Having seen four films which Sheridan previously directed -- My Left Foot (1989), The Field (1990), In the Name of the Father (1993), and The Boxer (1997) -- I admire so much what he has contributed to our understanding of the courage and determination which many of his characters somehow summon in response to severe hardship. They evince an integrity lacking in the adult characters in this film, with the possible exception of Mateo. As indicated, there are several reasons for my disappointment in In America. Nonetheless, the performances by Hounsou and the Bolger sisters almost -- not quite -- elevate this film above the inadequacies of its  narrative (i.e. gritty realities awkwardly juxtaposed with fantasy) as well as the disturbing questions raised about the Sullivans as parents.- not quite -- elevate this film above the inadequacies of its  narrative (i.e. gritty realities awkwardly juxtaposed with fantasy) as well as the disturbing questions raised about the Sullivans as parents.

1400052181|5|The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks by Rebecca Skloot is a story long overdue.  In parts, it was fascinating, heartbreaking, outrageous and frightening--but ultimately uplifting.  Skloot's book is actually several different stories, and she weaves them together seamlessly.  There is the story of Henrietta Lacks, her family, her cells and the ethics involved in medical research.<br /><br />Henrietta Lacks was a 30 year-old black woman when she was diagnosed with cervical cancer in 1951.  Living in Baltimore with her husband and 5 children, she sought treatment at Johns Hopkins University Hospital.  She died 9 months later.  Before her death, her doctor took samples of her cells--both normal and cancerous.  The cells were given to Hopkins researcher, Dr. George O. Gey.  Gey was not only able to keep the cancerous cells alive, but they actually started reproducing--the first time this was seen in human cells.  Gey was generous with these cells and before long, researchers from all over the world were using Henrietta's cells (now called HeLa) for research.  Many scientists claim that HeLa cells were the most important medical breakthrough in the 20th Century.<br /><br />Over the years, HeLa cells \\"helped develop drugs for treating herpes, leukemia, influenza, hemophilia, and Parkinson's disease; and they've been used to study lactose digestion, sexually transmitted diseases, appendicitis, human longevity, mosquito mating, and the negative cellular effects of working in sewers.\\"  They've been exposed to radiation, and used to test \\"steroids, chemotherapy drugs, hormones, vitamins, and environmental stress...\\"  They've been sent into space and used to test cosmetics.  They've been used to study genetics, they've been cloned, and they were used to create hybrids.  The U.S. Patent and Trademark Office lists over 17,000 patents using HeLa.  Today, HeLa cells have evolved into a multibillion dollar business.<br /><br />It wasn't until over 20 years after Henrietta's death that her family became aware that her cells were still alive, being used for research and making companies lots of money.  When the author was 16, her science teacher introduced Skloot to Henrietta Lacks and her cells, although he didn't know much about Henrietta.  So Skloot decided that Henrietta's story needed to be told.  But Henrietta's family had major reservations about Skloot and her motives and at first, refused to work with her.  \\"It's not only the story of HeLa cells and Henrietta Lacks, but of Henrietta's family--particularly Deborah [daughter]--and their lifelong struggle to make peace with the existence of those cells, and the science that made them possible.\\"  It also seems terribly wrong to them that while many have become rich off their mother, they are poor, uninsured, and can't afford the drugs that were most likely created using HeLa cells.<br /><br />The Immortal Life also deals with medical ethics, consent, medical experiments on humans, cell ownership, and who has a right to profit from your cells.  The tissues taken from Henrietta were taken without her consent.  Some of her cancer cells were injected into unknowing patients in medical experiments.  There have been gains made over the years to protect patients, but there is always a battle between patients and researchers when changes are proposed.<br /><br />The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks is a book that will haunt you long after you're done.  I can't recommended it enough.e aware that her cells were still alive, being used for research and making companies lots of money.  When the author was 16, her science teacher introduced Skloot to Henrietta Lacks and her cells, although he didn't know much about Henrietta.  So Skloot decided that Henrietta's story needed to be told.  But Henrietta's family had major reservations about Skloot and her motives and at first, refused to work with her.  \\"It's not only the story of HeLa cells and Henrietta Lacks, but of Henrietta's family--particularly Deborah [daughter]--and their lifelong struggle to make peace with the existence of those cells, and the science that made them possible.\\"  It also seems terribly wrong to them that while many have become rich off their mother, they are poor, uninsured, and can't afford the drugs that were most likely created using HeLa cells.      The Immortal Life also deals with medical ethics, consent, medical experiments on humans, cell ownership, and who has a right to profit from your cells.  The tissues taken from Henrietta were taken without her consent.  Some of her cancer cells were injected into unknowing patients in medical experiments.  There have been gains made over the years to protect patients, but there is always a battle between patients and researchers when changes are proposed.    The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks is a book that will haunt you long after you're done.  I can't recommended it enough.

B006LSZECO|4|I'm a fan of books that emphasize personal interactions and relationships.  Not only that, but the best books are those that provide unique insights into personalities and relationships.  I've read dozens (hundreds?) of books that focus on these themes, many of them quite good, but relatively few that provide sheer shock value.  \\"Gone Girl\\" falls into the \\"shock\\" category.  My first thought, after completing the book, was that I'd never encountered someone like Amy, but on second thought I realized I knew someone just like her, so her personality wasn't an author's fantasy.  I'm treading on thin ice here, as I don't want to be accused of providing a plot spoiler.  So I'll have to leave it at that.    Speaking of plot spoilers, I'm sometimes irritated by extravagantly long, specific reviews that give a blow-by-blow account of an entire book.  I know, I know.  If I want to read a book with no preconceived notions, the solution is not to search the reviews.  But I can't resist (and actually rely on) sampling a few before deciding to buy and read any book.  Some reviewers may think that so long as they don't tell what's on the final 3.5 pages, they aren't really revealing the plot.  But I think the plot of any fiction work begins on page one, then continues to evolve.  In this case, a number of reviewers said they didn't like the ending.  I'm sorry I read those comments, as they caused me to contemplate, as I read the last third of the book, what the egregious ending might be.  I would rather have sailed along and been surprised by the final twist.  (Now I'm in league with all those who show but not tell.)  :-)    Murder, marriage, childhood, coming of age -  always valid subjects for enjoyable reading, especially if the author gives the reader something special to think about.  \\"Gone Girl\\" definitely gives the reader plenty of food for thought.  A marriage in trouble is a common theme, but the Nick/Amy marriage is something else.  It leads to the question:  How well can one ever know another person?  Never having been married, I certainly can't pontificate on that subject as related to couples.  I do wonder, though, how many marriages survive 20, 30, 40, 50 years with the two parties still getting along, and even if they still survive the 50% divorce rate, do they get to know one another any better over time?  As I apply that question to my own relationship to friends and relatives, I must admit that knowing someone very well can sometimes lead to areas of discord.  How well do I actually know this person?  How well does s/he know me?  Well, I don't think anyone can know the whole \\"me.\\"  Perhaps I can't even know myself.  If that sounds off the subject of a review, my answer is that \\"Gone Girl\\" gave me the impetus to think about such things, for which it deserves a 4.5-star rating.

0767834739|5|While I felt the first and third movies in this trilogy were entirely average, this second part of the trilogy is nothing short of excellent. Here's why.    This movie breaks the mold of survival horror movies. The usual formula places a group of characters together and kills them off one at a time over the course of the movie. In Resident Evil: Apocalypse, the story progression is much more open ended. We follow the stories of several different groups of characters in different parts of a city sealed off from the outside world to contain the outbreak of a virus. While many characters do die, in this case, it is happpening to unique groups of people all making their own way to survive in a city over-run by zombies. Their storylines eventually converge of course, but the storyboarding developed to bring about this connection is stellar. As icing on the cake, all of these characters feel excellently cast and developed.    There are special effects in this movie, but they do not overpower the realism of human acting. The zombies, and most importantly, the Nemesis monster, are real actors in elaborate costumes and makeup.     The directing and set design are also excellent. This movie feels like a horror movie whereas the other two simply pass as alright action movies. The sets are intricately designed, with the very smallest attention to detail throughout. They are dark, filled with well placed streams of light and shadow, and dirty looking, in direct opposition to the clean, slick nature of the first movie. Everything really feels much closer to what the games envisioned in this outing. The directing is outstanding, filled with inspired camera angels, and the knowledge that true horror does not come from throwing scary beasts at us, but through the unknown about any scenario. The church scene is particularily inspired, giving us only the smallest glimpse of three attackers stalking the characters being portrayed. The level of suspense is masterfully built slowly to aclimax around wonderful camera angels and the mood of the set. It is also no wonder why the acting in the movie feels so superb as well. It may not be the stuff of oscars, but it is outstanding by action/horror standards. A great director can bring out the best talent from their actors and actresses. Another classic scene portrays frantic citizens of Racoon city trying to leave through the one exit in the barrier surrounding the city until one man at the front of the line succumbs to infection and the exit is shut tight for good.    Resident Evil: Apocalypse is an A grade movie, and by far the best of the series. If only all three were this good, and the director of this movie had been brought back for the third installment. It isn't a perfect film, but it is excellent in all the most important places. If you only buy or see one movie out of this trilogy, see this one. It is expertly crafted.

B0027MJU00|5|I love dark stories with what others might consider (and have described in other reviews here as) unlikeable characters. I actually did like all of the characters; most of them had at least one redeeming quality that I could identify with, and those who didn't (Diondra, Runner and Trey) were still entertaining and were of course integral to the story.<br /><br />I won't give away the ending, but spoilers ahead:<br /><br />The story starts in the present with the main character, Libby, learning that she is almost out of money that she received over the years as a charity case due to being the only surviving member of the gruesome murder of the rest of her family by her oldest brother twenty five years earlier. Just in the nick of time, she is approached by a member of a group of people who are fans of murder cases, and don't believe it was her brother who did it, and are willing to pay her to investigate the truth. Most of the members of the group are initially hostile towards Libby, since they are sympathetic towards her brother and it was her testimony that put him in prison.<br /><br />Each chapter alternates between different characters in the past on the day of the murder, and present day Libby as she progresses in her investigation over the course of what must be several weeks. She is very flawed; sarcastic, not friendly, she steals inexpensive things for no real reason other than she wants to at the time... but she's somehow also honest and open to these experiences that she's been avoiding for so long. I enjoyed her voice.<br /><br />Others have stated that the coincidences that culminate in the novel's climax are hard to believe or accept; I disagree. It is fiction, so we can agree that it didn't really happen, but coincidences do indeed happen all the time and I can accept this ending with no problem. I think the author did a great job.<br /><br />This is in no way a &#34;feel good&#34; book. B00004BZ0N|5|Jack Black spouts this in the recent film, 'School of Rock', as he sees school kids taking over a bus, and it's funny to many people, but some may scratch their heads. Why? Simply because the image of punk rock today is so distorted. It's albums like this where you really see how trashed the name punk became and what the hell IS punk rock.<br />'London Calling' is a vertebrae for all that is different in the genre so aptly labeled, punk. It has a melodic rock sound, it has reggae/rhythmic vibes, and it's definitely not the same each time around. Strummer Inc. made something that was basically a road sign that took you off the main highway. From the title track forward, the album is a raw slam with lessons and literature.<br />From 'Guns of Brixton' to 'Rudie Can't Fail', I mean, where do you not see difference? When everyone mentions punk and the godfathers behind it, they never mention Bowie, The Who, or even Cash, they always mention the now brothers and siblings of punk, Ramones and Sex Pistols. What makes The Clash so different from them is that this isn't the usual taste of punk, it's rock and roll daring to be different...which is what punk is in general. If everyone's wearing greasy hair, the kid with the hat is more punk rock than the bar chords of today.<br />See that's why 'London Calling' is so exceptional. It's such a farce in today's standards that punk rock is the ridiculous truckers' hats sideways and the shorts all mocked up;in actuality it's a fad that everyone does, contradicting what it is! It's laughable if you know where I'm coming from. In conclusion, punk rock is rare...and it's certainly not what it's called today. You want originality, theory, and difference? 'London Calling' is, no pun intended, your calling.<br />Exceptional is an understatement.

0517189542|5|The first time I read Louisa May Alcott's Little Women, I fell in love with the story.  Little Women is the story of four girls that were coming of age during the civil war.   Each of the girls, all sisters, has different personality traits and characteristics that are developed throughout the book. Meg, the oldest, is the sensible sister, while Jo is hot-headed and independent. Beth is musical and Amy, the youngest, is the more material of the four. The girls grew up in a very close family and strived to support each other in their dreams. But Jo, the second-born, has a difficult time seeing all of them growing up and leaving home. She wants things to remain the same, always. Change is inevitable, however, and throughout the book, the girls' love for each other is strong, as they face different challenges and joys of growing up. They keep their strong sense of family... I thoroughly enjoyed reading Little Women.  It has been awhile since I was able to sit down and read a book I so love.  Louisa May Alcott's character, Meg, did not hold much interest for me... Amy, the baby of the family, was too materialistic for my taste.  The character I related to the most was Jo.  I do not know if it is because I am like her, or if it was her spunk that I really liked.  I loved to see the blossoming love Laurie had for Jo... I think that Louisa May Alcott did a wonderful job in writing Little Women. I could relate to the book and with how the four sisters were at home with their mother and their father was off doing military things.  I grew up with a father in the Navy and he was gone a lot.  My mom, brother and I had to take care of things while he was gone.  Life goes on even if the whole family is not together.  <BR>  I fell in love with the Little Women at a young age and I hope to read this book to my children as they get older...  This is a great book for teaching these things to children.

Edit (8/27/2014): A couple of weeks ago, my G13 finally malfunctioned due to the hours and hours of use I subjected it to. I took it as an opportunity to order the Razer Orbweaver for comparison purposes. After 60+ hours of use of the Orbweaver, I concluded that I still prefer the Logitech G13 and ordered a second one. For those of you wondering why I didn't like ther Orbweaver, here is the review I wrote after two months of use:    \\"I have used both the Orbweaver and the Logitech G13 for prolonged gaming sessions (40+ hours logged on each). While the Orbweaver has mechanical buttons (so nice!), the design of the thumb stick renders it imprecise/impossible to use for anything other than camera or movement. The customization options for the ergonomics is nice, but I didn't find that if made much of a difference in my comfort or ease of use. The Razer Synaspe program necessary to program the keys is a pain to use and there is no macro recording on the fly.     Personally, I preferred the functionality and comfort of the Logictech G13 to the Orbweaver. The G13 has more programmable buttons and the thumb stick is more functional for how I use it. The G13 is also significantly cheaper and has a progammable LCD. The Logitech Gaming Software is much easier to use and more flexible in functionality due to being able to directly code using LUA. While the G13 is not mechanical, I have not had any problems gaming with it.    If mechanical keys are a deal-breaker, the Orbweaver will fulfill your requirements, but it is my opinion that the Logitech G13 is the better overall product for the above listed reasons. If only Logitech would release a mechanical G13. . . \\"

B000AL730O|5|Forty years ago this month, the Beatles released Paul McCartney's melancholic, autumnal \\"Yesterday\\" as a single in the United States.  Between the June '65 Yesterday/I've Just Seen a Face/I'm Down recordings through the March '67 She's Leaving Home sessions, Paul was at his musical peak, expanding the musical palette of popular music and focusing on the images he wanted to convey in his lyrics. <br /> <br />This brings us to Paul's newest release - \\"Chaos and Creation in the Backyard.\\"  Having listened to it in its entirety this evening at home, I can say that this album, taken as a thematic and unified whole, rivals Revolver and Pet Sounds and George's All Things Must pass, and may narrowly stand above Sgt. Pepper/The White Album/Abbey Road/Band on the Run/Imagine/Ringo.  <br /> <br />I know this statement is bound to cause controversy.  Let me explain.  First, with this album, McCartney has had his first real partner who could veto/censor/modify his ideas since John L. and (early) George M. four decades ago.  Second, this album is both introspective/autobiographical and universal at the same time.  In many ways, \\"Chaos\\" is the mature, adult response to \\"Pet Sounds\\" whereas \\"Pepper\\" took on \\"Pet Sounds\\" musically all those years ago. <br /> <br />In terms of tone and timbre, this album closely resembles the acoustic/piano textures of the quieter tracks on the White Album, McCartney, and Ram.  The prinicpal difference, which makes this music MATTER, is the four decades of life experiences Paul has had since those halcyion and bygone days.  If in the White Album, the Beatles were in the autumn of there musical career together,\\"Chaos\\" finds Paul in the autumn of his life - the shadows starting to lengthen, mortality facing him squarely in the eye, the ghosts of friends and lovers now long gone haunting this man.  By any standard, this album is more than mere pop music, it qualifies as musical and compositional expression of the highest order.  After all, McCartney's recording career now spans an awe-inspiring FORTY-SEVEN YEARS (dating from the 1958 McCartney/Harrison recording \\"In Spite of All the Danger.\\"  Listen carefully to the music and the lyrics - you can hear, in the shadows, the bittersweet and now long lost harmonies of John, George, and Linda in this beautifully haunting music.   <br /> <br />Just like \\"A Day in the Life,\\" Paul here confronts the sadness and difficulties of real life and still believes, as we should all believe, in love and hope.  Not a bad message for a world grown weary by war and natural disasters this September, 2005.

B000002J1I|3|It comes as a surprise to me that despite this album's reputation, I've listened to this one more than I ever listened to 1977's 'Going For The One'.<br />It's not that Tormato is an infinitely better album or more accessible than Going For The One. I find that Tormato has songs I really like, songs I desperately try to like, and songs that just leave me baffled as to the direction some were going.<br />SONGS I REALLY LIKE:<br />Release,Release to me is a precursor to DRAMA (1980). I hear in this track the direction Howe/Squire/White wanted to go with YES for the 80's. It has been said that the head of Atlantic heard this song from the demos, and thought that if every song followed suit, a great YES album for the new decade approaching was on its way. Unfortunately, Tormato is not Release, Release. You can definitely hear throughout the album that there are two different directions being pursued, one by Howe/Squire/White and one by Anderson/Wakeman. That they united at all is the mystery.<br />'On The Silent Wings of Freedom' is the other song I really enjoy, strong emphasis both on rhythm and unity in the Howe/Squire/White trio. I hear Drama being born on this album, and some of the members realising that the 80's were coming, and others resisting change and forcing a direction that seems has left many fans high and dry in dissatisfaction or puzzlement as to why???<br />'Onward' rests outside these two songs as my other favourite. The reviewer who has stated it may be better suited for Squire's solo album, I have to agree. It does more suit 'Fish Out of Water' than on here, but its still a beautiful song nevertheless, and one of the best things Squire has written, ever.<br />SONGS I DESPERATELY TRY TO LIKE:<br />Future Times/Rejoice , Don't Kill The Whale, and Madrigal. Each song has its strong points, but its on these songs I hear that struggle between two different factions (Howe/Squire/White vs. Anderson/Wakeman) most clearly, and where definitely someone's trying to win. They all have their moments, and though Madrigal is mainly Anderson/Wakeman, it seems a continuation of 'Turn of the Century' from Going For The One, a direction Anderson & Wakeman were definitely heading. After YES were 'classical' in a Beethoven/Stravinsky/Mozart way, it seems natural to explore Baroque music in the YES context, but I think it was too late in YES's career to go Baroque. Back in the Fragile days would have been better suited. The problem is, Anderson & Wakeman were of like mind, but 4 years too late. With Wakeman's departure in 1973, Anderson & Wakeman were as far apart as can be. 4 years later they agreed on the direction, but continuing a course started in 1973 by 1977/1978 seems better suited for a solo project and not in a group situation.<br />SONGS THAT JUST LEAVE ME BAFFLED:<br />Though I think the message of 'Circus of Heaven' I appreciate, that despite all of these wonderful creations mankind produces, the most beautiful seem to be those nature provides (if thats the message at all!!!!) this song seems all wrong for Yes to be doing. I like the fact that a slight reggae feel is incorporated into Yes music, but in 1978 EVERYONE discovered reggae! Well, except for Marley, Tosh, Cliff and all the originators of the genre. 'Arriving U.F.O.' suffers the same fate unfortunately.<br />I have no clue what Anderson & Wakeman wanted Yes to be at this point. It started on Going For The One, and ended disastrously on Tormato. Whether Baroque, or Reggae, or New Wave, whatever they saw, it was in no way prepared for the 80's, and had Howe, Squire & White allowed Anderson & Wakeman to pursue this direction unchallenged, you'd just be hearing about a YES Reunion tour being put together. Yes would have been finished in 1978 or a year later. Whenever I hear the Anderson/Wakeman/Howe/Squire/White grouping ,I always hear a tug and pull going on in the direction of the music. Someone always steps back, someone always stands upfront, its never in themiddle or compromise never pursued. I think every album these 5 people worked on suffers in a way because of it. You also notice that the 4 major works they co-operated on (Tales From Topographic Oceans / Going For The One / Tormato / and 'Keystudio') seem to be the albums that cause the greatest disagreement between Yes members AND Yes fans. It has to tell you that something's not quite right with this 5 man line-up.<br />Buy Tormato for the 3 great songs on here, or the 2 really disliked ones. Its up to you of course. But know that Yes have done far far better than this, and usually one or two of the members that produced this album AREN'T involved.dle or compromise never pursued. B00NAKWUGY|5|Like other users I thought My vinyl arrived defective and had skipping issues all over the first track and has numerous skips throughout the album. When I read their reviews, I instantly thought that I too had a defective record and was quick to write a review on the defect. I thought I had a defective album and was really bummed. I have a mid-1970s record player and listen to older records (with the exception of this one) and have never had a problem with skipping before. Then I did some reading and found that new albums are slightly different than my vintage albums. The skipping in my case was not a defective album, but user error. I was able to adjust the pressure on my needle for my record player (adjusted to be lighter on the record, the heavier weight was causing the skips) and just like that, my album was no longer skipping! I hope this helps other customers, because I suspect that all other users who also had skipping issues could remedy them by adjusting their record player as I had done. Sometimes, we just have to accept that there is a degree with user error.

B000ZLFALI|1|I believe that there are two kinds of people who will like this movie: teenagers who are desperate to find some kind of meaningful rebellion in their own lives (and earn themselves a tragic love story in the process), and people who thrive on pretension like plants thrive on sunlight.  In fact, I am convinced it was a team of these types of people who made this horrible movie in the first place.    The story centers on Jude and Lucy, who, at the beginning of the movie, are both saying goodbyes to that special someone in their lives: Jude, to his girlfriend, as he is going to America; Lucy, to her boyfriend who is shipping off to Vietnam.  Jude tells his girlfriend that he will \\"alway be true\\" (SLEDGEHAMMER OF FORESHADOWING), as Lucy's boyfriend assures her he will return from the war unscathed (ICE PICK OF FORESHADOWING APPLIED DIRECTLY TO THE CRANIUM WITH MAXIMUM FORCE).    Jude comes to America in search of his biological father, who is a custodian at Princeton.  Once Jude finds his father, there's a heartwarming reunion, right?  Nope, his father serves as the excuse for Jude to meet Max, a soon-to-be college drop-out and Lucy's brother.    Of course Lucy and Jude meet and fall in love while interacting with various characters who flit in and out of the story for seemingly no reason except to inspire Beatles puns (Oh, look, there's Prudence the lesbian who \\"came in through the bathroom window!\\", and the \\"Sexy\\" singer, Sadie!) or to tie in events that otherwise would not directly affect the characters already represented (Jojo, the black guitarist, becomes important only when the screenwriters want to shoehorn \\"black\\" events into the script, like the Detroit riots and the death of Dr. Martin Luther King).    There is very little plot.  Events are barely strung together (\\"Dear Prudence\\" sung as a plaintive ode to the forlorn character of the same name, a psychedelic road trip that ends at a Terry Gilliam-esque circus for no apparent reason, a bar room concert that is possibly the least frenetic and seizure inducing bit of the whole film), before Max is shipped off to Vietnam and Lucy, fearing he will meet the same fate as her brother, becomes radically political while Jude immerses himself in his art.  They grow apart, but the viewer finds it difficult to really care, as the only indication given that they are in any sort of relationship is the fact that they gaze soulfully at each other and float nearly nude in a watery blue void.      Of course it all ends Happily Ever After (tm).  Or not.  The viewer doesn't know, as the film closes with Lucy standing on an adjacent rooftop, again gazing soulfully toward Jude.  Her pout is indiscernible, but the music crescendos to let the audience know that they have just experienced some deep universal truth.  The rest of characters' stories are left unresolved as well, unless the viewer wishes to take the time to create their endings for them.    That's one of the main problems with the movie.  It assumes that you will make the connections of the plot yourself.  One moment, Prudence is locked in the closet.  The next, the characters are bumping into her by surprise at the psychedelic circus, where she has seemingly been working for a while.  Jude believes something tragic has happened to Lucy, but he seeks her out without the viewers being shown how his misconception has been cleared up in the first place.  The film is content to careen on from concept to concept without the messy legwork of logistics and plot development.    The biggest crime perpetrated by this film and its makers is the outright abuse of the Beatles catalog.  Fans of the Beatles music (that is to say, fans who did not come into familiarity with the material through this movie alone) know that each song has a context in the vast Beatles mythology, a context which the filmmakers merrily toss aside in favor of bending the pieces to fit their trite version of a love story.  In the worst of cases, lyrics are interpreted literally, making them absurd (underwear clad soldiers march across a miniature Vietnam carrying the Statue of Liberty on their backs while grunting \\"She's So Heavy\\").  Other songs, such as \\"Helter Skelter\\" and \\"While My Guitar Gently Weeps\\" are used to illustrate events that have no correlation to their meaning.    I would like to say that the cast does what they can with the shoddy material they were given to act, but that would be a lie.  They do what they can with the mediocre talent they were given.  Evan Rachel Wood spends much of the movie gazing deeply at things with a pained expression.    Jim Sturgess veers between incorrigible school boy and wounded puppy.  Joe Anderson manages to inject some depth into Max, but he's mostly upstaged by the absolutely asinine dance sequences he's surrounded by in the recruitment office and VA hospital.  Dana Fuchs rasps and vamps her way through the movie as if she's auditioning for a Janis Joplin bio-pic.     It boggles my mind that anyone could give this atrocious film a positive review of any kind.  When I left the theatre, I demanded my money back.  Then I was reminded it was a free screening and, as I could not get the moments of my life back that I lost while watching \\"Across The Universe\\", I feel I deserve to have my memory erased as a fair trade.    \\"Across The Universe\\" is the perfect movie for anyone who felt that \\"Forrest Gump\\" was too in-depth and realistic in its portrayal of the events of the sixties.  Anyone with an appreciation of acting, character and plot development and respect to historical events will just be disappointed.

Nope.<br /><br />\\"Saw\\" is a hack-job of a movie, cribbed from superior scripts like \\"Se7en\\" and \\"The Cube,\\" where victims get lured into totally illogical situations in some psychopath's attempt at teaching them a lesson. Items removed from water are also dry. Blood appears on victim's clothing and disappears. And in one of the most hysterically ridiculous gaffs in modern movies, a truck involved in the chase scene changes MODELS by the end of the chase!<br /><br />OK so I sat through the whole thing two times. But the movie's concept is relatively slick - enough to lure in scared patrons to two sequels and make Tobin Bell's Jigsaw one of the better movie villains in recent horror genres. (Incidentally, I feel \\"Saw 2\\" to be the best of the three films, and \\"Saw 3\\" a shade better than the original if only by benefit of better budget and better acting.) But if you take your horror/suspense movies seriously, you'll understand just how \\"Scary Movie 4\\" made hash out of \\"Saw\\" with barely an effort.4\\" made hash out of \\"Saw\\" with barely an effort.
B000002U9Y|5|Nobody believed they could do it--not even their original management, who abandoned them in favor of Syd Barrett, who now found it impossible to function within the band due to problems that included drugs.  \\"Jugband Blues\\" definitely captures the state he was in, as well as (I think) awareness of his being phased out.  Although I personally don't like this song, it certainly sets the stage for the rest of the album, which aside from one amazing and unique feature, forecasts Pink Floyd's later directions with great accuracy.<br />I feel that Pink Floyd's survival after this blow is owed not to one person, but TWO, who could hardly be more opposite: Richard Wright and Roger Waters.  One might even (if you allow me a bit of poetic license with my usage of the terms) call them \\"yin\\" and \\"yang\\" respectively.  With new guitarist David Gilmour still trying to find his place in the band, having not yet developed an independent style, quiet-natured Richard Wright brought his already-established Eastern-tinged and innovative musical talents to the table.  Even Roger Waters' \\"Set the Controls for the Heart of the Sun\\" owes a heavy debt to his keyboarding.  Mr. Wright also makes extensive songwriting contributions, including the gorgeous, haunting \\"Remember a Day\\" and the relaxing (NOT boring, thank you very much!) \\"See-Saw\\", both of which he wrote on his own.  Also remarkable is his vocal contribution.  The remaining half of the excellent Barrett-Wright vocal duo very nearly made his soft, melodic, and unique voice THE voice of Pink Floyd here.  In fact, to my ear, he made it onto *every* track except \\"Jugband Blues\\", although the title track is arguable (duet, Mr. Gilmour, or Mr. Wright?) and \\"Set the Controls\\" is hard to tell.  All I can say on the latter is, compare the faint \\"aaaahh\\" bit to the same place on Live at Pompeii where Mr. Gilmour is clearly doing it, or Ummagumma Live, where Mr. Waters does it, to the \\"aaaahh\\" at the beginning of \\"Far from the Harbour Wall\\" onRick Wright's Broken China, and tell me which the studio version sounds more like.<br />Pink Floyd's \\"yang\\" force, the outspoken Roger Waters, brought his lyrical skills to the table with lyrics to \\"Corporal Clegg\\" (shades of The Wall?), \\"Set the Controls\\" (\\"Will he remember the lesson of giving?\\"), and I think also \\"Let There Be More Light\\".  However, Mr. Waters seems, oddly enough, the more tentative of the opposite RW's on this album, perhaps because he was still working on finding his voice.  On ASoS he relies heavily on outside influences such as Syd Barrett and Chinese poetry.  That said, however, his is a worthy contribution.  <br />Nick Mason fans get a rare chance to hear him in full Eastern-psychedelic glory in the studio setting, most notably on \\"Set the Controls\\" and the apparent forerunner of Echoes (far more so than \\"Atom Heart Mother\\"), \\"A Saucerful of Secrets\\".  Lyrically, all three writers are turning away from the Piper-era whimsy towards nostalgia(in Mr. Wright's case) and madness (in Mr. Waters and Mr. Barrett's cases), although some of the psychedelic, free-form style remains.<br />Those of you who have not heard Piper or Relics may be shocked to hear the severe recording-equipment limitations the Floyd suffered from in their early days--this is down to their label, in my opinion, which underfunded them.  This, along with my dislike of \\"Jugband Blues\\" are why I dock the rating of this album half a star.  But as both are due to difficult, uncontrollable circumstances, and the band DOES come through them well, I give them the full 5 stars rather than rounding down.  Overall, this album is a must-have for any Pink Floyd fan's collection.  It was truly a unique moment in their history--never again would yin and yang experience such synergy with *both* virtually in \\"command\\".

B0000UJL96|5|\\"Star Trek VI:  The Undiscovered Country\\" is my favorite ST film.  Although I've only recently started watching the original series on G4 and TV Land, I've always had more of an attachment to the original crew than any of the others.  Since watching the old series, I've grown to love the original crew even more.  In \\"The Undiscovered Country,\\" our heroes find themselves on the edge of a brand new world in which the Klingons actually want (or for protection's sake, need) peace.  Naturally, Captain Kirk wants nothing to do with the Klingons on this matter.  However, Spock has \\"volunteered\\" the Enterprise to be the escort for the Klingon party to the new peace talks.  Things go wrong when the Chancellor Gorkon, the primary Klingon representative, is murdered on his own ship and it was seemingly done at the hand of the Enterprise.  What follows is a Klingon trial for Kirk and Bones, imprisonment on a Klingon penal mine planet, and Spock and the rest of the gang trying to uncover what's really going on.  It's a wonderful space mystery that I enjoy every time I see it.<br /><br />As for the DVD, it's a nice edition to anyone's collection.  It's full of nice documentaries ranging from a tribute to DeForest Kelley to a neat little segment known as \\"Penny's Toybox\\" in which we get to take a look at some of the props used in the film.  It was refreshing to see that not one of these documentaries (over three hours worth) included the typical self-serving bits as many DVDs include.  Ironically, this particular film could actually get away with documentaries where the crew pats themselves on the back, because this is one of the best \\"Trek\\" flicks ever.<br /><br />A wonderful send off to a wonderful space opera.  Highly recommended.

B0006A9FKA|5|As one might expect, SHAUN OF THE DEAD is a parodic remake of the classic George Romero film DAWN OF THE DEAD.  It pays frequent homage to the latter film through a host of direct references as well as its general subject matter (for instance, much of the \\"news\\" as read by the television presenters are lifted nearly word for word from Romero's script).  There differences are, however, marked.  Instead of an American location, this film is set in London.  And instead of holing up in a house to fight it out against the zombies, our heroes travel to a local pub.<br /><br />Although I thoroughly enjoyed this film, there is no question that not everyone finds this film that funny.  I'm not sure why that is, but I thought it would be fun to bat around some theories about why the reactions have been so diverse.  This is an important exercise for prospective viewers.  Are you more likely to side with those who find this to be a highly effective and funny farce or those who simply don't find anything funny at all?  So here are my speculations:<br /><br />First, I think some of those who do not like the movie failed to realize that this is an English comedy, with a very low-key, subdued approach to the story.  Before going to see this, I think a prospective viewer should answer is whether or not they enjoy MONTY PYTHON, Hugh Grant, THE OFFICE, and other British comic productions.  If the answer is \\"Yes\\" there is a high degree of probability that you will enjoy this one.  If the answer is \\"Absolutely not!\\" then you will almost certainly dislike this one.<br /><br />Second, I think some people, hearing the basic premise, imagine that this is going to be a bit like SCARY MOVIE or some other such parody of a genre.  I know for a fact that the couple sitting behind me in the viewing I went to had expectations along these lines.  They were expecting more out and out farce, a more frenetic pace, and far more jokes and cartoonish humor.  But because SHAUN OF THE DEAD is more deliberately paced, allows the absurd tale to unfold gradually, and the jokes rationed out more gradually, they were truly upset.<br /><br />I love British humor so I definitely fall more into the class of people apt to enjoy this.  I loved the pacing and subdued reactions of the main characters.  I loved, for instance, a pair of scenes that are allowed to unfold rather slowly.  The first occurs rather early in the movie when Shaun leaves his apartment and out of sheer habit walks across the street and down the sidewalk to the local corner store where he buys a couple of items.  The very same path is followed the next morning, with Shaun without thinking (almost zombie-like?) covers the same path to the store, utterly unaware of how dramatically everything has changed, of all the people he had seen the day before who had been transformed into zombies or been killed.  They were superbly executed scenes, and it is hard to imagine any American studio allowing a scene in a zombie comedy film to unfold so nicely.<br /><br />There are many wonderful scenes in the film, some very short and some a bit longer.  I loved, for instance, the store employee who interrupted a meeting to accept a call on his cell phone.  Or the extraordinary scene later in the film when, to get through a group of zombies, the group of friends mimic the behavior of zombies in order to get past them.  Their rehearsing their imitations of zombies was one of the best scenes in the film.  Bill Nighy, who pretty much stole the show (despite being part of an ensemble cast) in LOVE ACTUALLY as the burned out rock musician, is in a number of nice moments in a regretfully small part.<br /><br />I really loved the great teamwork between stars Simon Pegg and David Frost.  Both had worked together before on TV in SPACED, and Pegg co-wrote the screenplay with director Edgar Wright, also of SPACED.  The cast as a whole is quite strong, though I did find the character of David to be just a tad too irritating.<br /><br />All in all, I really had a great time seeing this one and I would strongly recommend it to anyone who enjoys British humor.  But if overly broad, in-your-face American style farce along the lines of SCARY MOVIE is what you want, you'd better skip this one.and I would strongly recommend it to anyone who enjoys British humor.  But if overly broad, in-your-face American style farce along the lines of SCARY MOVIE is what you want, you'd better skip this one.

0064471837|5|Sabriel literally returned me to reading. I was an avid reader as a youth and teen. As I progressed through higher education I read less and less for enjoyment (as I was constantly reading for work). I picked up Sabriel at the library in 1999 in the middle of writing my PhD dissertation as a much needed breather from scientific papers and research reports. Little did I know that it would essentially provide reading material for years (even still). I loved Sabriel and the writing of Mr. Nix enough that I went looking for other things authored by him. What I found on that early Internet was a list of books that had influenced him. Many were some of my favorites from my youth (Lloyd Alexander, Robert Heinlein, Susan Cooper, Ursula Le Guin, & Joan Aiken), but there were many I had never heard of.<br /><br />After a great journey through some of the old greats and newly revealed to me stuff I discovered that some of the books I enjoyed most as a child just didn't hold up very well now. So it was with a bit of trepidation that I picked up Sabriel again a few months ago to do a re-read 14 years or so after  the first time. This is a very uncharacteristic thing for me as I can count on 1 hand the books I've ever re-read. There are too many good books to be going back! I am pleased to say that 14 years was enough to remember the love I had for the book, but forget some of the details so that rereading was still a bit mysterious.<br /><br />Actual review starts here: I am one of those people who refuse to read the back of the book or book cover leaf because I don't want to have anything spoil or prejudice me before I read it for myself. Consequently my reviews are very limited on details of the book, focusing rather on how it made me feel so I don't spoil it for you. Let me just say that I like Mr. Nix's characters. Sabriel is someone that, even though she is a particular type of Necromancer,  I can relate to. She makes decisions like a real person would. Nothing puts me off a book faster than stupid characters who do things that only someone with a rock for a brain would do just to move the story along. Above all else I think this is what I enjoyed about Sabriel. I could enjoy imagining The Old Kingdom where magic not only works, but is embodied in small, smart-mouthed cats (& friendly dogs...). I believed the trials Sabriel faced because there was no sense of a sure outcome. People died while making great sacrifices, others died because they were in the wrong place at the wrong time. My favorite books and movies through the years have always been those that are realistic in this at least. Magic, walking dead, bells named after beings of great power that exert that power on the living and dead - all this I can accept as long as actions have realistic consequences and nobody is miraculously saved in a manner that is not feasible within the structure of the story.<br /><br />Well - I didn't do so well on the details, but trust me. Buy and read this book. As a faithful library user I rarely buy books. I've purchased two sets of this trilogy because I recommend them and loan them often. Sabriel is a true treat that has a story that is interesting from the first pages, that stays interesting throughout, that keeps the reader dreaming and imagining life \\"over the wall.\\" Indeed it is one of those books that makes me believe that someplace there may be a wall separating us from our neighbors who live a very different life.a book faster than stupid characters who do things that only someone with a rock for a brain would do just to move the story along. If you look at the background, you can see the traffic is moving on the left.<br /><br />Now you might say it's a European car so it should be as shown in the film. But no. Japan like UK requires imports to be adapted for left side driving. The scenes of closeup of the driver and passenger talking to each other have the background traffic dubbed in. I am guessing that to US watchers, this could completely pass them by. Everything seems as it should for the closeups. Better would have been the use of a left sided car. If nothing else, it subtly emphasises that they are indeed in a different country. It is obvious with the Asian backdrop; but tweaking it via a correct car gives an authentic nuance. Especially because the driver is British, so he indeed is used to that driving.

0385491026|4|\\"And yet it disturbs me to know I have hurt someone unintentionally. I want all my hurts to be intentional.\\"<br /><br />A short quote, but biting, full of scorn and honesty. After re-reading it a few times, working the words over in my head, I came to a realization. I realized what this means.<br /><br />This is what girlhood is.<br /><br />And, really, underneath all of the middle-age self-misery and careless, wistful affairs with older art professors, \\"Cat's Eye\\" is all about how a woman's girlhood shapes the way she lives the rest of her life. The pain we feel follows us into adulthood, and the wounds we inflict leave their mark as well. We never want to cause those real wounds, but by God, we all did.<br /><br />Elaine Risley, a controversial painter, has returned to Toronto to attend a retrospective devoted to her work. While back in the city, Elaine reflects on her relationships and friendships she had as a child and teenager, especially the one she had with a girl named Cordelia, whose influence followed Elaine for her entire life.<br /><br />We see Elaine make a bold transition from a tomboyish little girl who traveled with her family all over Canada for her father's science career to a clueless school-aged girl who is thrust suddenly into the world of other school-aged girls. Girl culture is a prominent theme in this novel, the culture of fashion scrapbooks and makeup and stealing things from drugstores, of dating etiquette and relationships with brothers, of knowing your place in the hierarchy of female friendship. Atwood knows, as all women do, that the cruelest people on this plant are young girls. If you have ever been a female between the ages of 9 and 18, you have survived hell. This is also a novel that touches on human consumerism, a theme popular in many of Atwood's environmental/sci-fi novels--Elaine realizes that she needs things; she needs purses and nightgowns and makeup. In the crux of her relationship with these three cruel girls--Grace, Carol, and the worst perpetrator, Cordelia--Elaine starts consuming herself, chewing on her hair, biting the skin around her nails and the hard flesh on the bottoms of her feet. Elaine stays with these girls because as we all know, a childhood spent with awful friends is better than a childhood spent alone, especially for girls. There is a part of the novel where after the girls pick on Elaine, Elaine takes all of her hard-earned babysitting money, quits her babysitting job, and spends all the earnings on candy, which she gives to the girls. They consume it, and in a way, Elaine feels consumed, and she mistakes this for love. Girlhood literally eats away at a child until she is nothing but the product of her environment.<br /><br />Elaine hardens to this girl culture, becomes a sharpened and bored high-school girl with a \\"mean mouth,\\" not impervious to this culture of catty women but simply afraid of it. The most emotional parts of the novel are when Elaine recalls events with her older brother Stephen, when times were simpler and she could focus on the hands-on activities and tasks she liked without having to worry about what she looked like from behind or how she spoke. Elaine doesn't really trust women but she doesn't really trust men either--the hurts inflicted on her from those girls when they were kids destroyed her ability to trust anyone.<br /><br />This book is the \\"cat's eye\\" of Elaine's life, the entire scope of her experience, and it is an interesting read with rich detail, full of Atwood's sharp eye for the realities of being a girl. That is part of the problem, though--these characters are just like you and me, too similar, until you wait and wait for a climax and nothing ever really happens. This just isn't a very exciting book, which is disappointing, because many of Atwood's novels have left me completely breathless. I understand her growth and change as a writer--I am much more fond of her later novels than her earlier ones. Even though this was an ambitious, well-written work of a fiction, I guarantee I will forget almost everything about it within a month, because it was a hyper-realistic account of a person's experience--it wasn't really a story. I also know that some people really love this. I totally get it. It just wasn't what I was expecting.<br /><br />I'd still recommend it, though, and I think it might be interesting to read as a teenager and then again as an adult. I wonder if I will read this again someday and see if any of those girlhood wounds have healed.

B000002NJS|5|In terms of popular music of the late 20th century? Madonna's creative MO has always been best defined for me as challenging. Musically,personally and in many cases spiritually. She'd spent much of the 1990's doing exactly that,and gleefully taking her admirers/listeners along for the ride. During the middle of the decade? Life changed for her. She got married,divorced and had her daughter. One thing I remember about the year 1998 actually had a lot to do with the same concept. Musically there was a transition from the angrier punk style of alternative music to a trancier,more psychedelic one. For me? This was a very appealing transition. It was through the BMG music club about a year after this came out that I picked it up there. And each time I hear it? A new level of greatness emerges from it.<br /><br />  &#34;Drowned World/Substitute For Love&#34; is an ambient melodic delight that builds from instrumentally featherweight to heavyweight between each part. &#34;Swim&#34; is a bluesy electronic/hip-hop/dance number with some gurgling bass effects and rhythm guitar. The title song is a Hi NRG electronica/disco number that,like &#34;Sky Fits Heaven&#34;,&#34;Skin&#34; and the slower dub reggae style of &#34;Shanti/Ashtangi&#34; emphasis extremely tribal,Afrocentric rhythms within their arrangements. &#34;Candy Perfume Girl&#34;,&#34;Nothing Really Matters&#34;,&#34;The Power Of Goodbye&#34;,&#34;To Have And Not To Hold&#34; and the beautiful Arabic style of &#34;Frozen&#34; are all slower trip hop type rhythms. &#34;Little Star&#34; is an easy going drum n bass type number with a round bass line while &#34;Mer Girl&#34; is a hauntingly orchestrated ballad closer.<br /><br />  To use one of the biggest cliche among writers and critics? This album is Madonna's Sgt. Peppers. And I say that with every bit of honesty and sincerity I can possibly manage. None of the musical territory Madonna explored here was totally new and innovative when this came out. What she did in the company of producers such as Patrick Leonard,Marious De Vries and especially William Orbit was to create a melodically and lyrically vivid world which could match the musical nervous system this created. You can dance to this,but it's far from easy pop. Love,loss,birth,death,fear and joy all weave throughout this album in a way I really never quite heard from Madonna after or since. In a way? It was the creative culmination of where she'd been heading from 1982 to this point. B008SAZHLQ|4|At first I thought this book was drawn out and a bit annoying, but after finishing I realized that the author did a great job when it comes to capturing the attitudes and actions of teenagers. Furthermore, the revelation near the end of the novel about the writing on Eleanor's book really shed more light (or darkness?) on her being guarded, since the environment she was in was far worse than we'd thought due to underlying and unspoken hardships and threats that were a constant in her world.<br /><br />The uncertainty and need for repeated confirmation in Eleanor and Park's romance was getting on my nerves at one point but when I thought about it, I think the author has it right and it's shocking that many of these aloof behaviors and self-conscious actions are wildly present in us as teenagers, but even still as adults too. It drove me crazy how Park and Eleanor both cared deeply for each other, and knew the other felt the same, but still had moments of awkwardness, uncertainty, and even put up walls. Again, this is something that spans beyond teenage years but is more prevalent then.<br /><br />Also, the intensity they have for each other, that grew from adorable and humble beginnings (and comic books) is spot on for their age and I love how well it's captured in this novel. I think most people can think of at least one person we thought was the absolute greatest but were still afraid to fully let them have all-access to more of the person we were/are.<br /><br />I'm a sucker for a young romance story because couples I know who've been together for decades and they still flirt, smile, romance, and try to win each other over. They seem to have never lost that little spark of teenage love-sickness and it's fantastic!!<br /><br />While there is a great story in this book, it's mostly a character-driven novel and despite their quirks, annoying aloofness, uncertainty, and struggles, I think you'll love Eleanor & Park.
